## Chapter 9: Fourier Series and Transforms:




### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of uniform electromagnetic plane waves. These waves are a fundamental concept in the study of electromagnetic energy, and understanding them is crucial for comprehending the operation of a wide range of devices, from motors to lasers.

Uniform electromagnetic plane waves are a type of electromagnetic wave where the electric and magnetic fields are perpendicular to each other and to the direction of propagation. They are characterized by their wavelength, frequency, and amplitude, and are governed by Maxwell's equations.

We will explore the properties of these waves, including their propagation, reflection, and absorption. We will also discuss the concept of polarization, where the electric field vector of a plane wave oscillates in a fixed plane. This property is crucial for many applications, such as the operation of polarizers and the generation of circularly polarized light.

Furthermore, we will examine the interaction of uniform electromagnetic plane waves with various materials and structures. This includes the reflection and transmission at interfaces, the diffraction at apertures, and the interaction with antennas.

Finally, we will discuss some practical applications of uniform electromagnetic plane waves, such as the generation of microwaves, the operation of radar systems, and the use of lasers in various fields.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of uniform electromagnetic plane waves and their role in the world of electromagnetic energy. This knowledge will serve as a foundation for the subsequent chapters, where we will explore more complex phenomena and applications.




#### 9.1a Antenna Overview

Antennas are a crucial component in the generation and reception of electromagnetic waves. They are responsible for converting electrical energy into electromagnetic waves and vice versa. In this section, we will provide an overview of antennas, discussing their types, characteristics, and applications.

#### Types of Antennas

There are several types of antennas, each with its unique characteristics and applications. The most common types include:

- Dipole antennas: These are the simplest type of antennas and are widely used in many applications. They consist of two metal rods or wires of equal length, positioned end to end with a small gap in between. The feed line is connected to the gap. Dipole antennas are omnidirectional in the plane perpendicular to the axis of the antenna.

- Monopole antennas: These are a type of antenna that consists of a single rod or wire, usually mounted over a ground plane. They are commonly used in mobile phones and wireless communication systems.

- Yagi-Uda antennas: These are directional antennas that are widely used in television and radio broadcasting. They consist of a dipole antenna with additional elements, known as directors and reflectors, which are used to improve the antenna's directivity.

- Parabolic antennas: These are high-gain, directional antennas that are used in many applications, including satellite communication and radar systems. They consist of a parabolic reflector and a feed antenna at its focus.

#### Antenna Characteristics

The performance of an antenna is characterized by several parameters, including:

- Gain: This is a measure of the antenna's ability to direct energy in a particular direction. It is usually expressed in decibels (dB).

- Bandwidth: This is the range of frequencies over which the antenna can operate effectively.

- Impedance: This is a measure of the opposition to the flow of current in a circuit. In antennas, it is a measure of the antenna's ability to match the impedance of the transmission line to which it is connected.

- Polarization: This is the orientation of the electric field of the antenna's radiated wave. It can be linear (vertical or horizontal) or circular (right-hand or left-hand).

#### Applications of Antennas

Antennas are used in a wide range of applications, including:

- Wireless communication: Antennas are used in all types of wireless communication systems, including mobile phones, Wi-Fi, and satellite communication.

- Radar systems: Antennas are used in radar systems to transmit and receive electromagnetic waves.

- Radio broadcasting: Antennas are used in radio broadcasting to transmit radio signals.

- Television broadcasting: Antennas are used in television broadcasting to transmit television signals.

- Satellite communication: Antennas are used in satellite communication to transmit and receive signals between satellites and ground stations.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the principles of antenna operation, discussing the theory behind antenna design and performance. We will also discuss the design and analysis of specific types of antennas, including dipole antennas, Yagi-Uda antennas, and parabolic antennas.

#### 9.1b Antenna Design

The design of an antenna is a complex process that involves careful consideration of the antenna's type, characteristics, and applications. The design process typically involves the following steps:

1. **Specification**: The first step in antenna design is to define the specifications of the antenna. This includes the frequency range, gain, bandwidth, impedance, and polarization of the antenna. These specifications are typically determined by the application of the antenna.

2. **Selection of Antenna Type**: Based on the specifications, an appropriate type of antenna is selected. For example, if the application requires a high-gain, directional antenna, a parabolic antenna might be chosen.

3. **Design of Antenna Elements**: The design of the antenna elements, such as the dipole, monopole, or Yagi-Uda elements, is then carried out. This involves determining the dimensions of the elements, their spacing, and their placement.

4. **Analysis of Antenna Performance**: The performance of the antenna is then analyzed using mathematical models and simulations. This involves calculating the gain, bandwidth, impedance, and polarization of the antenna.

5. **Optimization of Antenna Performance**: Based on the analysis, the antenna design is optimized to improve its performance. This might involve adjusting the dimensions of the antenna elements, changing the type of antenna, or adding additional elements.

6. **Testing of Antenna Performance**: Finally, the performance of the antenna is tested using physical prototypes. This involves measuring the gain, bandwidth, impedance, and polarization of the antenna.

The design of antennas is a complex field that requires a deep understanding of electromagnetic theory, materials science, and electronics. However, with the right knowledge and tools, it is possible to design antennas that meet a wide range of specifications and applications.

#### 9.1c Antenna Applications

Antennas, due to their ability to convert electrical energy into electromagnetic waves and vice versa, find applications in a wide range of fields. This section will explore some of the key applications of antennas.

1. **Wireless Communication**: Antennas are a crucial component of wireless communication systems. They are used to transmit and receive electromagnetic waves, enabling the transfer of information over long distances without the need for physical cables. Antennas are used in various wireless communication technologies, including Wi-Fi, Bluetooth, and cellular networks.

2. **Radar Systems**: Radar systems use antennas to transmit and receive electromagnetic waves. The antennas are used to generate a beam of electromagnetic waves that is transmitted into the air. When these waves encounter an object, they are reflected back towards the radar system. The antenna is also used to receive the reflected waves, which can then be analyzed to determine the location and speed of the object.

3. **Satellite Communication**: Antennas are used in satellite communication systems to transmit and receive signals between satellites and ground stations. The antennas are used to transmit signals from the ground station to the satellite and to receive signals from the satellite back to the ground station.

4. **Radio Broadcasting**: Antennas are used in radio broadcasting to transmit radio signals. The antennas are used to convert the electrical signals into electromagnetic waves that can be transmitted through the air.

5. **Television Broadcasting**: Antennas are used in television broadcasting to transmit television signals. The antennas are used to convert the electrical signals into electromagnetic waves that can be transmitted through the air.

6. **Radio Astronomy**: Antennas are used in radio astronomy to detect and study radio waves from celestial bodies. The antennas are used to receive the radio waves from the celestial bodies and to convert them into electrical signals that can be analyzed.

7. **Microwave Ovens**: Antennas are used in microwave ovens to generate and receive microwaves. The antennas are used to generate a beam of microwaves that is used to heat food. They are also used to receive the microwaves that are reflected back from the food, which can then be analyzed to determine the temperature of the food.

These are just a few examples of the many applications of antennas. The design and optimization of antennas for these applications is a complex process that requires a deep understanding of electromagnetic theory, materials science, and electronics.




#### 9.1b EM Wave Generation

The generation of electromagnetic waves is a complex process that involves the interaction of electric and magnetic fields. In the previous section, we discussed the role of antennas in this process. In this section, we will delve deeper into the generation of electromagnetic waves, focusing on the role of antennas and the principles of electromagnetics.

#### Antenna Design and Analysis

The design and analysis of antennas is a crucial aspect of electromagnetic wave generation. As mentioned earlier, antennas are responsible for converting electrical energy into electromagnetic waves and vice versa. The design of an antenna involves careful consideration of its size, shape, and placement in the electromagnetic field.

The analysis of antennas, on the other hand, involves studying their performance in terms of parameters such as gain, bandwidth, and impedance. This is typically done using mathematical models and simulations, which can provide valuable insights into the behavior of the antenna in the electromagnetic field.

#### Antenna Types and Characteristics

As mentioned earlier, there are several types of antennas, each with its unique characteristics and applications. The choice of antenna type depends on the specific requirements of the application, including factors such as frequency range, bandwidth, and directivity.

The characteristics of an antenna, such as its gain, bandwidth, and impedance, are also important considerations. These parameters can be optimized to achieve the desired performance in the electromagnetic field.

#### Antenna Arrays

In some applications, multiple antennas can be used together to form an antenna array. This can provide improved performance in terms of gain, bandwidth, and directivity. Antenna arrays are commonly used in radar systems, satellite communication, and wireless communication.

#### Antenna Design and Analysis Tools

The design and analysis of antennas can be a complex task, requiring a deep understanding of electromagnetics and the ability to use various tools and techniques. These include mathematical models, simulations, and experimental methods.

Mathematical models, such as Maxwell's equations, provide a theoretical framework for understanding the behavior of electromagnetic waves. Simulations, using software tools such as HFSS and CST, can provide a more detailed and realistic representation of the antenna's performance in the electromagnetic field. Experimental methods, such as measurements and testing, can provide valuable data for validating the design and analysis.

In the next section, we will discuss the role of antennas in the generation of electromagnetic waves, focusing on the principles of electromagnetics and the design and analysis of antennas.

#### 9.1c Antenna Applications

Antennas have a wide range of applications in various fields, including telecommunications, broadcasting, radar systems, and satellite communication. In this section, we will explore some of these applications in more detail.

#### Telecommunications

In telecommunications, antennas are used to transmit and receive electromagnetic waves. They are an integral part of the communication infrastructure, enabling the transmission of voice, data, and video signals over long distances. The design and analysis of antennas are crucial in this field, as they directly impact the quality and reliability of the communication service.

#### Broadcasting

Antennas are also used in broadcasting, particularly in television and radio broadcasting. They are used to transmit electromagnetic waves to a wide audience. The design of antennas in this field is often complex, as it involves balancing various factors such as coverage, bandwidth, and interference.

#### Radar Systems

Radar systems rely heavily on antennas for their operation. Antennas are used to transmit and receive electromagnetic waves, which are then used to detect and track objects. The design of antennas in radar systems is critical, as it directly impacts the system's performance in terms of range, resolution, and accuracy.

#### Satellite Communication

In satellite communication, antennas are used to transmit and receive electromagnetic waves between satellites and ground stations. The design of these antennas is particularly challenging, as they need to operate over a wide range of frequencies and conditions.

#### Other Applications

Antennas are also used in other applications such as wireless power transfer, RFID systems, and medical imaging. In these fields, the design and analysis of antennas are crucial for achieving optimal performance.

In conclusion, antennas play a vital role in many areas of modern technology. Their design and analysis are complex and require a deep understanding of electromagnetics. As technology continues to advance, the role of antennas is likely to become even more significant.




#### 9.1c Antenna-Wave Relationship

The relationship between antennas and electromagnetic waves is a fundamental aspect of electromagnetics. This relationship is governed by Maxwell's equations, which describe how electric and magnetic fields interact to produce electromagnetic waves.

#### Antenna Radiation Patterns

The radiation pattern of an antenna is a graphical representation of the radiation properties of the antenna as a function of space coordinates. It is typically represented in the form of a three-dimensional plot or a two-dimensional cross-sectional plot. The radiation pattern provides valuable information about the directional characteristics of the antenna, including its gain, beamwidth, and sidelobe level.

The radiation pattern of an antenna is determined by the antenna's physical characteristics, such as its size, shape, and placement in the electromagnetic field. It is also influenced by the frequency of the electromagnetic wave.

#### Antenna Impedance

The impedance of an antenna is a measure of how much it resists the flow of electromagnetic energy. It is a complex quantity that includes both the resistance and reactance of the antenna. The impedance of an antenna is a critical parameter in the design and analysis of antennas. It affects the efficiency of the antenna in converting electrical energy into electromagnetic waves and vice versa.

The impedance of an antenna is typically represented as a complex number, $Z = R + jX$, where $R$ is the resistance and $X$ is the reactance. The resistance represents the power that is lost in the antenna, while the reactance represents the power that is stored in the antenna.

#### Antenna Bandwidth

The bandwidth of an antenna is the range of frequencies over which the antenna can operate effectively. It is a critical parameter in the design and analysis of antennas. The bandwidth of an antenna is typically defined as the range of frequencies over which the antenna's performance, such as its gain and impedance, remains relatively constant.

The bandwidth of an antenna is influenced by its physical characteristics, such as its size, shape, and placement in the electromagnetic field. It is also affected by the frequency of the electromagnetic wave.

#### Antenna Gain

The gain of an antenna is a measure of its ability to direct electromagnetic energy in a particular direction. It is a critical parameter in the design and analysis of antennas. The gain of an antenna is typically defined as the ratio of the intensity, in a given direction, to the intensity that would be obtained if the power accepted by the antenna were radiated isotropically.

The gain of an antenna is influenced by its physical characteristics, such as its size, shape, and placement in the electromagnetic field. It is also affected by the frequency of the electromagnetic wave.

#### Antenna Beamwidth

The beamwidth of an antenna is the angular width of the main lobe of the antenna's radiation pattern. It is a critical parameter in the design and analysis of antennas. The beamwidth of an antenna is typically defined as the angular width between the half-power (-3 dB) points of the main lobe.

The beamwidth of an antenna is influenced by its physical characteristics, such as its size, shape, and placement in the electromagnetic field. It is also affected by the frequency of the electromagnetic wave.

#### Antenna Sidelobe Level

The sidelobe level of an antenna is the level of the first sidelobe of the antenna's radiation pattern. It is a critical parameter in the design and analysis of antennas. The sidelobe level of an antenna is typically defined as the level of the first sidelobe relative to the main lobe of the radiation pattern.

The sidelobe level of an antenna is influenced by its physical characteristics, such as its size, shape, and placement in the electromagnetic field. It is also affected by the frequency of the electromagnetic wave.

#### Antenna Efficiency

The efficiency of an antenna is a measure of how effectively it converts electrical energy into electromagnetic waves and vice versa. It is a critical parameter in the design and analysis of antennas. The efficiency of an antenna is typically defined as the ratio of the power radiated by the antenna to the power accepted by the antenna.

The efficiency of an antenna is influenced by its physical characteristics, such as its size, shape, and placement in the electromagnetic field. It is also affected by the frequency of the electromagnetic wave.

#### Antenna Polarization

The polarization of an antenna is a measure of how the electric field vector of the antenna's radiation pattern varies with position. It is a critical parameter in the design and analysis of antennas. The polarization of an antenna is typically defined as the orientation of the electric field vector of the antenna's radiation pattern.

The polarization of an antenna is influenced by its physical characteristics, such as its size, shape, and placement in the electromagnetic field. It is also affected by the frequency of the electromagnetic wave.

#### Antenna Directivity

The directivity of an antenna is a measure of how much the antenna concentrates its radiation in a particular direction. It is a critical parameter in the design and analysis of antennas. The directivity of an antenna is typically defined as the ratio of the intensity, in a given direction, to the average intensity radiated by the antenna.

The directivity of an antenna is influenced by its physical characteristics, such as its size, shape, and placement in the electromagnetic field. It is also affected by the frequency of the electromagnetic wave.

#### Antenna Bandwidth

The bandwidth of an antenna is the range of frequencies over which the antenna can operate effectively. It is a critical parameter in the design and analysis of antennas. The directivity of an antenna is influenced by its physical characteristics, such as its size, shape, and placement in the electromagnetic field. It is also affected by the frequency of the electromagnetic wave.

#### Antenna Bandwidth

The bandwidth of an antenna is the range of frequencies over which the antenna can operate effectively. It is a critical parameter in the design and analysis of antennas. The bandwidth of an antenna is typically defined as the range of frequencies over which the antenna's performance, such as its gain and impedance, remains relatively constant.

The bandwidth of an antenna is influenced by its physical characteristics, such as its size, shape, and placement in the electromagnetic field. It is also affected by the frequency of the electromagnetic wave.

#### Antenna Gain

The gain of an antenna is a measure of its ability to direct electromagnetic energy in a particular direction. It is a critical parameter in the design and analysis of antennas. The gain of an antenna is typically defined as the ratio of the intensity, in a given direction, to the intensity that would be obtained if the power accepted by the antenna were radiated isotropically.

The gain of an antenna is influenced by its physical characteristics, such as its size, shape, and placement in the electromagnetic field. It is also affected by the frequency of the electromagnetic wave.

#### Antenna Beamwidth

The beamwidth of an antenna is the angular width of the main lobe of the antenna's radiation pattern. It is a critical parameter in the design and analysis of antennas. The beamwidth of an antenna is typically defined as the angular width between the half-power (-3 dB) points of the main lobe.

The beamwidth of an antenna is influenced by its physical characteristics, such as its size, shape, and placement in the electromagnetic field. It is also affected by the frequency of the electromagnetic wave.

#### Antenna Sidelobe Level

The sidelobe level of an antenna is the level of the first sidelobe of the antenna's radiation pattern. It is a critical parameter in the design and analysis of antennas. The sidelobe level of an antenna is typically defined as the level of the first sidelobe relative to the main lobe of the radiation pattern.

The sidelobe level of an antenna is influenced by its physical characteristics, such as its size, shape, and placement in the electromagnetic field. It is also affected by the frequency of the electromagnetic wave.

#### Antenna Efficiency

The efficiency of an antenna is a measure of how effectively it converts electrical energy into electromagnetic waves and vice versa. It is a critical parameter in the design and analysis of antennas. The efficiency of an antenna is typically defined as the ratio of the power radiated by the antenna to the power


#### 9.2a Atomic Structure

The atomic structure is the arrangement of subatomic particles—protons, neutrons, and electrons—that make up an atom. The structure of an atom is crucial in understanding its properties and behavior, including its interaction with electromagnetic waves.

#### Electron Configuration

The electron configuration of an atom describes the arrangement of electrons in the atom's electron shells. The electron shells are energy levels around the nucleus where electrons are most likely to be found. The electron configuration is determined by the Pauli Exclusion Principle, which states that no two electrons in an atom can have the same set of quantum numbers.

The electron configuration of an atom can be determined using the Aufbau Principle, which states that electrons fill the lowest energy levels first. The Aufbau Principle is responsible for the order of filling of the electron shells in an atom.

#### Periodic Table

The periodic table is a tabular arrangement of chemical elements, organized based on their electron configurations. The periodic table is a useful tool for predicting the electron configurations of atoms and their chemical properties.

The periodic table is divided into groups and periods. The groups are horizontal rows, and the periods are vertical columns. The periodic table can be used to predict the electron configurations of atoms by looking at the electron configurations of the elements in the same group or period.

#### Electronegativity

Electronegativity is a measure of an atom's ability to attract electrons towards itself in a chemical bond. The Pauling scale and the Allen scale are two commonly used scales to measure electronegativity.

The Pauling scale is based on the concept of bond polarity, where a more electronegative atom will have a more negative partial charge and a less electronegative atom will have a more positive partial charge. The Allen scale, on the other hand, is based on the concept of electron affinity, where a more electronegative atom will have a higher electron affinity.

#### Electronegativity and Electron Configuration

The electron configuration of an atom plays a crucial role in determining its electronegativity. The electron configuration of an atom can be used to predict its electronegativity, as well as its chemical and physical properties.

For example, atoms with incomplete electron shells, such as oxygen and fluorine, tend to have higher electronegativity values due to the presence of unshared electrons. This is because unshared electrons are more easily attracted towards a more electronegative atom in a chemical bond.

On the other hand, atoms with complete electron shells, such as noble gases, tend to have lower electronegativity values. This is because noble gases have stable electron configurations and are less likely to attract electrons towards themselves in a chemical bond.

In the next section, we will explore how the electron configuration of an atom affects its interaction with electromagnetic waves.

#### 9.2b Interaction of Atoms with EM Waves

The interaction of atoms with electromagnetic waves is a fundamental aspect of quantum physics. This interaction is governed by the principles of quantum mechanics, which describe the behavior of particles at the atomic and subatomic level.

#### The Interaction of Atoms with Electromagnetic Waves

Atoms interact with electromagnetic waves in a variety of ways, depending on their electron configuration and the properties of the electromagnetic wave. The interaction can result in absorption, emission, or scattering of the electromagnetic wave.

#### Absorption

Absorption occurs when an atom absorbs an electromagnetic wave, causing an excitation of the atom. This excitation can result in a change in the electron configuration of the atom, leading to a change in the atom's properties. The energy of the absorbed electromagnetic wave must match the energy difference between the electron shells of the atom for absorption to occur.

#### Emission

Emission is the opposite of absorption. It occurs when an excited atom emits an electromagnetic wave, returning to a lower energy state. The energy of the emitted electromagnetic wave is equal to the energy difference between the electron shells of the atom.

#### Scattering

Scattering occurs when an electromagnetic wave interacts with an atom without being absorbed or emitted. The electromagnetic wave is deflected by the atom, changing its direction and possibly its wavelength. Scattering can occur without any change in the electron configuration of the atom.

#### Quantum Mechanics and the Interaction of Atoms with Electromagnetic Waves

The principles of quantum mechanics provide a mathematical description of the interaction of atoms with electromagnetic waves. The Schrödinger equation, a fundamental equation in quantum mechanics, describes the evolution of the wave function of an atom interacting with an electromagnetic wave.

The wave function, denoted by $\Psi$, is a mathematical function that describes the state of an atom. The square of the absolute value of the wave function, $|\Psi|^2$, gives the probability density of finding the atom in a particular state.

The interaction of atoms with electromagnetic waves is a complex topic that requires a deep understanding of quantum mechanics. However, the principles and equations described above provide a solid foundation for further study in this fascinating field.

#### 9.2c Applications of Atomic Interactions

The interaction of atoms with electromagnetic waves has a wide range of applications in various fields, including spectroscopy, lasers, and quantum computing.

#### Spectroscopy

Spectroscopy is a technique used to study the interaction of light with matter. It is based on the absorption, emission, and scattering of electromagnetic waves by atoms. The specific frequencies at which an atom absorbs or emits light are determined by the energy differences between its electron shells. These frequencies can be measured and analyzed to provide information about the atom's electron configuration and properties.

#### Lasers

Lasers, or Light Amplification by Stimulated Emission of Radiation, are devices that produce and amplify light. They operate by stimulating the emission of light from atoms, a process that is governed by the principles of quantum mechanics. The interaction of atoms with electromagnetic waves is crucial in the operation of lasers.

#### Quantum Computing

Quantum computing is a field that leverages the principles of quantum mechanics to perform computational tasks. Quantum computers use quantum bits, or qubits, which can exist in a superposition of states. The interaction of atoms with electromagnetic waves is used to manipulate these qubits, allowing quantum computers to perform complex calculations at a much faster rate than classical computers.

#### Quantum Mechanics and the Interaction of Atoms with Electromagnetic Waves

The principles of quantum mechanics provide a mathematical description of the interaction of atoms with electromagnetic waves. The Schrödinger equation, a fundamental equation in quantum mechanics, describes the evolution of the wave function of an atom interacting with an electromagnetic wave.

The wave function, denoted by $\Psi$, is a mathematical function that describes the state of an atom. The square of the absolute value of the wave function, $|\Psi|^2$, gives the probability density of finding the atom in a particular state. The interaction of atoms with electromagnetic waves can be described by manipulating the wave function.

In conclusion, the interaction of atoms with electromagnetic waves is a fundamental aspect of quantum physics with wide-ranging applications. Understanding this interaction is crucial for the development of technologies such as lasers and quantum computers.

In the previous chapters, we have explored the fundamental concepts of electromagnetic energy, from motors to lasers. We have delved into the principles of electromagnetism, the behavior of electromagnetic waves, and the applications of these phenomena in various fields. Now, we will embark on a journey to understand non-uniform electromagnetic plane waves.

Non-uniform electromagnetic plane waves are a fascinating aspect of electromagnetism. They are waves of electromagnetic energy that are not uniform in their distribution across the plane of the wave. This non-uniformity can be in the form of variations in the amplitude, phase, or polarization of the wave. 

In this chapter, we will explore the properties of non-uniform electromagnetic plane waves, their behavior, and their applications. We will delve into the mathematical representations of these waves, using the powerful language of vector calculus and Maxwell's equations. We will also explore the physical implications of these mathematical representations, such as the Poynting vector and the concept of energy flow.

We will also discuss the practical applications of non-uniform electromagnetic plane waves. These applications range from the design of antennas and radar systems, to the development of advanced imaging techniques, and even to the understanding of certain biological phenomena.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of non-uniform electromagnetic plane waves, from the basic principles to the most advanced applications. It is designed to be accessible to both students and professionals in the field of electromagnetism, and to provide a solid foundation for further exploration and research.

This will allow us to express complex mathematical concepts in a clear and concise manner.

#### 9.2b EM Wave Interaction

The interaction of electromagnetic waves with atoms is a fundamental concept in physics and engineering. This interaction is responsible for a wide range of phenomena, from the absorption of light by atoms to the emission of light in lasers.

#### Absorption Spectroscopy

Absorption spectroscopy is a technique used to study the interaction of electromagnetic waves with atoms. In this technique, a beam of light is passed through a sample of atoms, and the amount of light absorbed is measured as a function of the wavelength of the light. The resulting spectrum can then be compared to theoretical predictions or to spectra obtained from other samples to identify the atoms present in the sample.

The absorption of light by atoms is governed by the Beer-Lambert Law, which states that the absorbance of a sample is proportional to its concentration and the path length of the light through the sample. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
A = \epsilon \cdot c \cdot l
$$

where $A$ is the absorbance, $\epsilon$ is the molar absorptivity (a measure of how strongly an atom absorbs light), $c$ is the concentration of the sample, and $l$ is the path length of the light through the sample.

#### Emission Spectroscopy

Emission spectroscopy is another technique used to study the interaction of electromagnetic waves with atoms. In this technique, a sample of atoms is excited to a higher energy state, and the light emitted as the atoms return to their ground state is collected and analyzed. The resulting spectrum can then be compared to theoretical predictions or to spectra obtained from other samples to identify the atoms present in the sample.

The emission of light by atoms is governed by the principles of quantum mechanics. In particular, the energy of the emitted light is determined by the energy difference between the initial and final states of the atom, as given by the equation:

$$
\Delta E = h \nu
$$

where $\Delta E$ is the energy difference, $h$ is Planck's constant, and $\nu$ is the frequency of the light.

#### Laser Emission

Lasers are devices that produce coherent light through the process of stimulated emission. In a laser, atoms are excited to a higher energy state, and then stimulated to emit light as they return to their ground state. The emitted light is then amplified and focused to produce a beam of coherent light.

The interaction of electromagnetic waves with atoms is a crucial aspect of laser operation. The absorption of light by atoms is used to excite the atoms to a higher energy state, and the emission of light is used to amplify the light and produce the coherent beam. Understanding this interaction is therefore essential for understanding and designing lasers.

#### 9.2c Applications of EM Waves

The interaction of electromagnetic waves with atoms has a wide range of applications in various fields. These applications span from communication systems to medical imaging and from industrial processes to environmental monitoring.

#### Communication Systems

In communication systems, electromagnetic waves are used to transmit information over long distances. The interaction of these waves with atoms is crucial in the design and operation of these systems. For instance, in wireless communication, the absorption and emission of electromagnetic waves by atoms are used to transmit and receive signals. The Beer-Lambert Law is often used in the design of wireless communication systems to predict the absorption of signals by the atmosphere and other media.

#### Medical Imaging

In medical imaging, electromagnetic waves are used to create images of the human body. Techniques such as X-ray imaging and magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) rely on the interaction of electromagnetic waves with atoms to produce images. For example, in X-ray imaging, the absorption of X-rays by atoms in the body is used to create a shadow image. In MRI, the interaction of magnetic fields with atomic spins is used to create an image of the body.

#### Industrial Processes

In industrial processes, electromagnetic waves are used in a variety of applications, including heating, melting, and welding. The interaction of these waves with atoms is crucial in these processes. For instance, in induction heating, electromagnetic waves are used to heat metal objects without the need for physical contact. The absorption of these waves by the metal is used to generate heat, which can then be used for various industrial processes.

#### Environmental Monitoring

In environmental monitoring, electromagnetic waves are used to study the composition of the atmosphere and other environmental samples. Techniques such as spectroscopy and remote sensing rely on the interaction of electromagnetic waves with atoms to obtain information about the environment. For example, in spectroscopy, the absorption of light by atoms is used to identify the atoms present in a sample. In remote sensing, the interaction of electromagnetic waves with the atmosphere is used to obtain information about the atmosphere from a distance.

In conclusion, the interaction of electromagnetic waves with atoms is a fundamental concept with a wide range of applications. Understanding this interaction is crucial for the design and operation of various systems and processes.




#### 9.2c Interaction Effects

The interaction of electromagnetic waves with atoms can have a profound effect on the behavior of the atoms. These effects can be categorized into two main types: absorption and emission.

#### Absorption Effects

Absorption of electromagnetic waves by atoms can cause a variety of effects. One of the most significant is the excitation of the atoms from their ground state to a higher energy state. This is known as absorption spectroscopy, and it is a powerful tool for identifying the atoms present in a sample.

The absorption of light by atoms can also lead to ionization, where an electron is ejected from the atom. This can be particularly significant in high-energy scenarios, such as in the core of stars or in high-energy physics experiments.

#### Emission Effects

Emission of electromagnetic waves by atoms can also have significant effects. One of the most well-known examples is the emission spectrum, which is a characteristic pattern of light emitted by an atom when it transitions from a higher energy state to a lower one. This spectrum can be used to identify the atom, much like absorption spectroscopy.

Emission of light can also lead to the formation of new atoms, a process known as photoionization. This is particularly important in astrophysics, where it plays a key role in the formation of stars and other celestial bodies.

#### Interaction Effects in Quantum Mechanics

The interaction of electromagnetic waves with atoms is governed by the principles of quantum mechanics. In particular, the interaction can be described using the Schrödinger equation, which describes the evolution of the wave function of a quantum system.

The interaction of electromagnetic waves with atoms can also lead to quantum entanglement, a phenomenon where two or more particles become correlated in such a way that the state of one particle cannot be described without considering the state of the other particles. This effect has been observed in a variety of systems, including atoms and photons.

In conclusion, the interaction of electromagnetic waves with atoms is a complex and fascinating area of study. It has profound implications for our understanding of the physical world, and it has practical applications in a wide range of fields, from spectroscopy to quantum computing.

## Chapter: Chapter 10: Examples of Non-uniform EM Waves

### Introduction

However, the real-world scenarios are often more complex and dynamic, requiring us to understand and deal with non-uniform electromagnetic waves.

In this chapter, we will shift our focus to non-uniform electromagnetic waves. These waves are characterized by their non-uniformity in terms of their amplitude, frequency, and phase. Unlike uniform waves, non-uniform waves can exhibit a wide range of behaviors, making them more challenging to analyze and predict. However, understanding non-uniform waves is crucial for many practical applications, including radar systems, wireless communication, and medical imaging.

We will begin by defining what non-uniform electromagnetic waves are and how they differ from uniform waves. We will then explore some common examples of non-uniform waves, including Gaussian beams, Bessel beams, and Lorentzian beams. We will discuss their properties, how they are generated, and how they propagate. We will also look at how these waves can be manipulated and controlled for various applications.

Throughout this chapter, we will use mathematical models and equations to describe these non-uniform waves. For example, we might use the equation `$y_j(n)$` to represent the amplitude of a non-uniform wave at a given point in space and time. We will also use graphical representations to illustrate these concepts and make them more accessible.

By the end of this chapter, you should have a solid understanding of non-uniform electromagnetic waves, their properties, and their applications. You should also be able to apply this knowledge to practical problems and scenarios. So, let's dive into the fascinating world of non-uniform electromagnetic waves.




#### 9.3a Lossy Wave Definition

Lossy waves are a type of electromagnetic wave that experiences energy loss due to absorption and scattering as it propagates through a medium. This loss of energy can be due to various factors such as material properties, frequency, and the presence of impurities or defects in the medium. 

The propagation of lossy waves can be described by Maxwell's equations, which relate the electric and magnetic fields to the electric and magnetic polarizations of the medium. For lossy waves, these equations can be modified to account for the energy loss. 

The energy loss in lossy waves can be quantified by the attenuation constant, denoted by $\alpha$. The attenuation constant is a measure of how much the wave's amplitude decreases per unit length. It is typically expressed in units of nepers per meter (Np/m) or decibels per meter (dB/m).

The attenuation constant can be calculated using the following equation:

$$
\alpha = \frac{1}{\delta} \ln \left( \frac{I_0}{I} \right)
$$

where $I_0$ is the initial intensity of the wave, $I$ is the intensity at a distance $x$ from the source, and $\delta$ is the skin depth, which is a measure of the depth at which the wave's amplitude decreases by a factor of $e$.

The skin depth can be calculated using the following equation:

$$
\delta = \frac{1}{\sqrt{\pi f \mu_0 \mu_r \sigma}}
$$

where $f$ is the frequency of the wave, $\mu_0$ is the permeability of free space, $\mu_r$ is the relative permeability of the medium, and $\sigma$ is the conductivity of the medium.

The propagation of lossy waves can be further analyzed using the wave equation, which describes how the wave's electric and magnetic fields evolve over time and space. For lossy waves, the wave equation can be modified to account for the energy loss.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the propagation of lossy waves and explore the effects of different medium properties on the wave's behavior.

#### 9.3b Lossy Wave Analysis

The analysis of lossy waves involves understanding the factors that contribute to energy loss and how these factors affect the wave's propagation. This analysis can be complex due to the multitude of factors that can contribute to energy loss, including material properties, frequency, and the presence of impurities or defects in the medium.

One of the key factors that contributes to energy loss in lossy waves is the conductivity of the medium. As mentioned in the previous section, the conductivity $\sigma$ is a measure of how easily a medium can conduct electricity. For lossy waves, a higher conductivity means a greater energy loss.

The conductivity can be related to the attenuation constant $\alpha$ and the skin depth $\delta$ through the following equation:

$$
\alpha = \frac{1}{\delta} \ln \left( \frac{I_0}{I} \right) = \frac{1}{\delta} \ln \left( \frac{1}{e} \right) = \frac{1}{\delta}
$$

This equation shows that the attenuation constant is inversely proportional to the skin depth. This means that as the skin depth decreases (i.e., the wave's amplitude decreases more rapidly), the attenuation constant increases, indicating a greater energy loss.

Another factor that contributes to energy loss is the frequency of the wave. As the frequency increases, the energy loss also increases. This is due to the fact that higher frequency waves have shorter wavelengths, which means they interact more strongly with the medium and experience more energy loss.

The frequency can be related to the attenuation constant and the skin depth through the following equation:

$$
\alpha = \frac{1}{\delta} \ln \left( \frac{I_0}{I} \right) = \frac{1}{\delta} \ln \left( \frac{1}{e} \right) = \frac{1}{\delta}
$$

This equation shows that the attenuation constant is inversely proportional to the skin depth. This means that as the skin depth decreases (i.e., the wave's amplitude decreases more rapidly), the attenuation constant increases, indicating a greater energy loss.

The presence of impurities or defects in the medium can also contribute to energy loss. These impurities or defects can disrupt the wave's propagation and cause energy to be scattered or absorbed. The effect of these impurities or defects on energy loss can be complex and depends on their size, shape, and distribution in the medium.

In the next section, we will explore some specific examples of lossy waves and how these factors contribute to energy loss in different scenarios.

#### 9.3c Lossy Wave Applications

Lossy waves find a wide range of applications in various fields due to their unique properties. In this section, we will explore some of the key applications of lossy waves.

##### Electromagnetics

In electromagnetics, lossy waves are used in the design of antennas, transmission lines, and other components of electromagnetic systems. The understanding of lossy waves is essential in these applications due to the energy loss that occurs during wave propagation. For instance, in the design of antennas, the understanding of lossy waves helps in optimizing the antenna's performance by minimizing energy loss.

The attenuation constant $\alpha$ and the skin depth $\delta$ are key parameters in the design of these components. As we have seen in the previous section, the attenuation constant is inversely proportional to the skin depth. This relationship is used in the design of transmission lines to minimize energy loss.

##### Optics

In optics, lossy waves are used in the design of optical fibers and other optical components. The understanding of lossy waves is crucial in these applications due to the energy loss that occurs during light propagation. For instance, in the design of optical fibers, the understanding of lossy waves helps in optimizing the fiber's performance by minimizing energy loss.

The attenuation constant $\alpha$ and the skin depth $\delta$ are key parameters in the design of these components. As we have seen in the previous section, the attenuation constant is inversely proportional to the skin depth. This relationship is used in the design of optical fibers to minimize energy loss.

##### Quantum Mechanics

In quantum mechanics, lossy waves are used in the study of quantum systems. The understanding of lossy waves is essential in these applications due to the energy loss that occurs during wave propagation. For instance, in the study of quantum systems, the understanding of lossy waves helps in understanding the behavior of quantum systems under different conditions.

The attenuation constant $\alpha$ and the skin depth $\delta$ are key parameters in the study of quantum systems. As we have seen in the previous section, the attenuation constant is inversely proportional to the skin depth. This relationship is used in the study of quantum systems to understand the behavior of quantum systems under different conditions.

In conclusion, lossy waves play a crucial role in various fields due to their unique properties. The understanding of lossy waves is essential in these applications due to the energy loss that occurs during wave propagation. The key parameters in these applications are the attenuation constant $\alpha$ and the skin depth $\delta$.




#### 9.3b Lossy Wave Properties

Lossy waves exhibit several unique properties that distinguish them from non-radiative dielectric waveguides. These properties are primarily due to the energy loss experienced by lossy waves as they propagate through a medium.

##### Attenuation Constant

As mentioned in the previous section, the attenuation constant, denoted by $\alpha$, is a measure of the energy loss in lossy waves. It is a function of the frequency of the wave, the properties of the medium, and the skin depth. The attenuation constant can be calculated using the following equation:

$$
\alpha = \frac{1}{\delta} \ln \left( \frac{I_0}{I} \right)
$$

where $I_0$ is the initial intensity of the wave, $I$ is the intensity at a distance $x$ from the source, and $\delta$ is the skin depth.

##### Skin Depth

The skin depth, denoted by $\delta$, is a measure of the depth at which the wave's amplitude decreases by a factor of $e$. It is a function of the frequency of the wave, the properties of the medium, and the skin depth. The skin depth can be calculated using the following equation:

$$
\delta = \frac{1}{\sqrt{\pi f \mu_0 \mu_r \sigma}}
$$

where $f$ is the frequency of the wave, $\mu_0$ is the permeability of free space, $\mu_r$ is the relative permeability of the medium, and $\sigma$ is the conductivity of the medium.

##### Wave Equation for Lossy Waves

The propagation of lossy waves can be described by Maxwell's equations, which relate the electric and magnetic fields to the electric and magnetic polarizations of the medium. For lossy waves, these equations can be modified to account for the energy loss. The wave equation for lossy waves can be written as:

$$
\nabla^2 \mathbf{E} - \frac{1}{c^2} \frac{\partial^2 \mathbf{E}}{\partial t^2} = \mu_0 \sigma \frac{\partial \mathbf{E}}{\partial t}
$$

where $\mathbf{E}$ is the electric field, $c$ is the speed of light, and $\sigma$ is the conductivity of the medium.

##### Wave Propagation in Lossy Media

In lossy media, the wave propagation is affected by the energy loss. The wave amplitude decreases with the skin depth as the wave propagates through the medium. This results in a decrease in the wave intensity, which can be quantified by the attenuation constant. The wave propagation in lossy media can be described by the wave equation for lossy waves.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the propagation of lossy waves and explore the effects of different medium properties on the wave's behavior.

#### 9.3c Lossy Wave Applications

Lossy waves find a wide range of applications in various fields due to their unique properties. These applications leverage the energy loss in lossy waves to perform tasks such as energy transfer, signal processing, and material characterization.

##### Energy Transfer

One of the primary applications of lossy waves is in energy transfer. The energy loss in lossy waves can be harnessed to transfer energy from one location to another. This is particularly useful in applications such as wireless power transfer, where energy is transferred from a power source to a device without the need for physical contact. The attenuation constant, $\alpha$, plays a crucial role in this application as it determines the rate of energy loss.

##### Signal Processing

Lossy waves are also used in signal processing. The wave equation for lossy waves, $\nabla^2 \mathbf{E} - \frac{1}{c^2} \frac{\partial^2 \mathbf{E}}{\partial t^2} = \mu_0 \sigma \frac{\partial \mathbf{E}}{\partial t}$, can be used to process signals. The skin depth, $\delta$, and the attenuation constant, $\alpha$, can be used to control the signal processing.

##### Material Characterization

The properties of a medium, such as its conductivity, $\sigma$, and relative permeability, $\mu_r$, can be determined by studying the propagation of lossy waves. This is particularly useful in material characterization, where the properties of a material are determined by analyzing how it interacts with electromagnetic waves. The wave equation for lossy waves, along with the skin depth and attenuation constant, can be used to perform this analysis.

In conclusion, lossy waves, due to their unique properties, find a wide range of applications. The attenuation constant, skin depth, and wave equation for lossy waves are key to understanding and leveraging these applications.

## Chapter: Chapter 10: Examples of Non-uniform EM Plane Waves

### Introduction

In the previous chapters, we have explored the fundamental principles of electromagnetic energy, its generation, and its propagation. We have delved into the world of uniform electromagnetic plane waves, understanding their behavior and characteristics. However, in the real world, not all electromagnetic waves are uniform. This chapter, "Examples of Non-uniform EM Plane Waves," will introduce us to the fascinating world of non-uniform electromagnetic plane waves.

Non-uniform electromagnetic plane waves are waves whose properties vary in space. They are not constant throughout their propagation, unlike uniform waves. This variation can be in the form of changes in amplitude, frequency, or phase. Non-uniform waves are found in many practical applications, from radar systems to wireless communication. Understanding their behavior is crucial for designing and optimizing these systems.

In this chapter, we will explore several examples of non-uniform electromagnetic plane waves. We will learn how to describe these waves using mathematical equations, and how to predict their behavior. We will also discuss the physical implications of non-uniform waves, and how they differ from uniform waves.

We will start by introducing the concept of non-uniform waves, and discussing their properties. We will then move on to specific examples, such as non-uniform waves in a dielectric medium, and non-uniform waves in a magnetic medium. We will also discuss non-uniform waves in the presence of a magnetic field, and non-uniform waves in the presence of a current.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of non-uniform electromagnetic plane waves, and be able to apply this knowledge to practical applications. You will also have a deeper understanding of the principles of electromagnetic energy, and be better equipped to tackle more advanced topics in this field.

#### 9.3c Lossy Wave Applications

Lossy waves find a wide range of applications in various fields due to their unique properties. In this section, we will discuss some of the key applications of lossy waves.

##### Wireless Communication

Lossy waves are extensively used in wireless communication systems. The attenuation constant, $\alpha$, and the skin depth, $\delta$, are crucial parameters in the design and operation of these systems. For instance, in the IEEE 802.11ah network standards, the attenuation constant is used to determine the range of wireless communication. Similarly, the skin depth is used to optimize the design of antennas and other components of wireless communication systems.

##### Multiple-Prism Dispersion Theory

The multiple-prism dispersion theory, first published in 1987, is another application of lossy waves. This theory is used to design prismatic pulse compressors, which are essential in many applications, including laser systems and optical communication. The theory is based on the propagation of lossy waves through a medium, and it provides explicit second-order equations that can be directly applied to the design of these pulse compressors.

##### Digital Signal Processing

Digital signal processing (DSP) is another field where lossy waves find significant applications. DSP is used in a variety of applications, including speech coding and transmission in digital mobile phones, room correction of sound in hi-fi and sound reinforcement applications, analysis and control of industrial processes, medical imaging such as CAT scans and MRI, audio crossovers and equalization, digital synthesizers, and audio effects units. The propagation of lossy waves through a medium is a key concept in many of these applications.

##### FreeWave Technologies

FreeWave Technologies is a company that offers products that include a module, board-level, and enclosed radios for OEM and industrial end-user connectivity and computing applications. The design and operation of these products often involve the propagation of lossy waves through a medium, and the attenuation constant and skin depth are crucial parameters in this process.

##### Grid-Leak Detector

The grid leak detector is another application of lossy waves. This device is used in radio frequency (RF) circuits to measure the RF power. The attenuation constant and skin depth are important parameters in the design and operation of this device. For instance, tetrode and pentode tubes, which provide significantly higher grid input impedance than triodes, are often used in grid leak detectors.

In conclusion, lossy waves play a crucial role in a wide range of applications, from wireless communication to digital signal processing. Understanding the propagation of lossy waves through a medium is essential in many of these applications.

Non-uniform electromagnetic plane waves are a critical aspect of electromagnetics, playing a significant role in various fields such as telecommunications, radar systems, and medical imaging. Unlike uniform waves, which maintain a constant amplitude and phase across the wavefront, non-uniform waves exhibit variations in these properties. This chapter will provide a comprehensive study of non-uniform electromagnetic plane waves, their characteristics, and their applications.

We will begin by understanding the basic principles that govern non-uniform waves, including their generation and propagation. We will then delve into the mathematical representations of these waves, using the powerful language of Maxwell's equations. #### 9.4a Polarized Light

Polarized light is a fundamental concept in the study of electromagnetic energy. It refers to light waves whose oscillations are restricted to a single plane. This property is a result of the light wave's interaction with a medium, such as a polarizer, which can selectively transmit light waves with a specific polarization state.

##### Polarization States

The polarization state of a light wave is determined by the orientation of its electric field vector. If the electric field vector oscillates in a single plane, the light is said to be linearly polarized. If the electric field vector rotates in a plane, the light is said to be circularly polarized. If the electric field vector oscillates along a single axis, the light is said to be axially polarized.

The polarization state of a light wave can be represented by a complex number, known as the Jones vector, which is defined as:

$$
\begin{bmatrix}
E_x \\
E_y
\end{bmatrix}
$$

where $E_x$ and $E_y$ are the complex amplitudes of the electric field in the x and y directions, respectively.

##### Polarizers

Polarizers are devices that can selectively transmit light waves with a specific polarization state. They are used in a variety of applications, including sunglasses, 3D glasses, and optical filters.

The transmission of a polarizer can be described by a 2x2 matrix, known as the Jones matrix, which is defined as:

$$
\begin{bmatrix}
T_{xx} & T_{xy} \\
T_{yx} & T_{yy}
\end{bmatrix}
$$

where $T_{xx}$ and $T_{yy}$ are the transmission coefficients for light waves with electric field vectors in the x and y directions, respectively, and $T_{xy}$ and $T_{yx}$ are the cross-transmission coefficients.

The Jones matrix of a polarizer is typically diagonal, with $T_{xx} = T_{yy}$, and $T_{xy} = T_{yx} = 0$. This means that the polarizer transmits light waves with electric field vectors in the x and y directions with equal efficiency, and does not transmit light waves with electric field vectors in the 45° and 135° directions.

##### Polarization Ellipse

The polarization state of a light wave can also be represented by a polarization ellipse. This is a graphical representation of the polarization state, where the major axis of the ellipse represents the direction of the electric field vector, and the length of the major axis represents the amplitude of the electric field.

The polarization ellipse can be constructed from the Jones vector and the Jones matrix of a polarizer. The major axis of the ellipse is given by the eigenvector of the Jones matrix, and the length of the major axis is given by the eigenvalue of the Jones matrix.

In the next section, we will discuss the properties of polarized light and how they can be manipulated using polarizers.

#### 9.4b Polarizers and Polarization

Polarizers play a crucial role in manipulating the polarization state of light waves. They are used in a variety of applications, including the reduction of glare from reflective surfaces, the enhancement of contrast in images, and the control of light in optical instruments.

##### Polarizer Transmission

The transmission of a polarizer is determined by its Jones matrix. As mentioned earlier, the Jones matrix of a polarizer is typically diagonal, with $T_{xx} = T_{yy}$, and $T_{xy} = T_{yx} = 0$. This means that the polarizer transmits light waves with electric field vectors in the x and y directions with equal efficiency, and does not transmit light waves with electric field vectors in the 45° and 135° directions.

The transmission of a polarizer can be represented by a complex number, known as the Jones factor, which is defined as:

$$
J = T_{xx} + jT_{xy}
$$

where $T_{xx}$ and $T_{xy}$ are the transmission coefficients for light waves with electric field vectors in the x and y directions, respectively, and $j$ is the imaginary unit.

The Jones factor can be used to calculate the transmission of a polarizer for any given polarization state. The transmission is given by the product of the Jones factor and the Jones vector of the incident light wave.

##### Polarization State after Passing through a Polarizer

The polarization state of a light wave after passing through a polarizer can be calculated from the Jones matrix of the polarizer. The Jones matrix acts as a projector onto the transmission plane of the polarizer. This means that the Jones vector of the transmitted light wave is given by the projection of the Jones vector of the incident light wave onto the transmission plane.

The Jones vector of the transmitted light wave is given by:

$$
\begin{bmatrix}
E_{tx} \\
E_{ty}
\end{bmatrix}
$$

where $E_{tx}$ and $E_{ty}$ are the complex amplitudes of the electric field in the x and y directions, respectively, after passing through the polarizer.

The polarization state of the transmitted light wave is determined by the ratio of the complex amplitudes $E_{tx}$ and $E_{ty}$. If $E_{tx} = E_{ty}$, the light wave is linearly polarized. If $E_{tx} = -E_{ty}$, the light wave is circularly polarized. If $E_{tx} \neq E_{ty}$, the light wave is elliptically polarized.

##### Polarization State after Passing through Two Polarizers

The polarization state of a light wave after passing through two polarizers can be calculated from the Jones matrices of the two polarizers. The Jones matrix of the second polarizer acts as a projector onto the transmission plane of the second polarizer. This means that the Jones vector of the transmitted light wave is given by the projection of the Jones vector of the incident light wave onto the transmission plane of the second polarizer.

The Jones vector of the transmitted light wave is given by:

$$
\begin{bmatrix}
E_{tx} \\
E_{ty}
\end{bmatrix}
$$

where $E_{tx}$ and $E_{ty}$ are the complex amplitudes of the electric field in the x and y directions, respectively, after passing through the second polarizer.

The polarization state of the transmitted light wave is determined by the ratio of the complex amplitudes $E_{tx}$ and $E_{ty}$. If $E_{tx} = E_{ty}$, the light wave is linearly polarized. If $E_{tx} = -E_{ty}$, the light wave is circularly polarized. If $E_{tx} \neq E_{ty}$, the light wave is elliptically polarized.

#### 9.4c Polarizer Applications

Polarizers have a wide range of applications in various fields due to their ability to manipulate the polarization state of light waves. In this section, we will discuss some of the key applications of polarizers.

##### Polarizers in Optical Instruments

Polarizers are essential components in many optical instruments, including polarizing microscopes, polarizing filters, and polarizing prisms. These instruments use the polarization properties of light to enhance contrast, reduce glare, and improve image quality.

For example, in a polarizing microscope, a polarizer is used to selectively transmit light waves with a specific polarization state. This allows the microscope to observe the birefringence of a sample, which is a measure of how much the refractive index of a material changes with the polarization state of light.

##### Polarizers in Imaging and Photography

Polarizers are also used in imaging and photography to reduce glare and improve image contrast. In photography, polarizers are often used to reduce reflections from non-metallic surfaces, such as glass and water. This can improve the color saturation and contrast of the image.

In addition, polarizers are used in imaging systems to control the polarization state of light. For example, in digital cameras, polarizers are used to control the polarization state of the light that enters the camera. This can improve the image quality and reduce the effects of lens flare.

##### Polarizers in Telecommunications

In telecommunications, polarizers are used to control the polarization state of light waves in optical communication systems. This is important because the polarization state of light can affect the propagation and reception of light waves in optical fibers.

For example, in optical communication systems, polarizers are used to control the polarization state of light waves at the transmitter and receiver. This can improve the signal quality and reduce the effects of polarization-dependent loss in the optical fibers.

##### Polarizers in Laser Systems

Polarizers are also used in laser systems to control the polarization state of laser light. This is important because the polarization state of laser light can affect the performance of the laser and the interaction of the laser light with other components in the system.

For example, in laser systems, polarizers are used to selectively transmit laser light with a specific polarization state. This can improve the beam quality and reduce the effects of polarization-dependent loss in the laser system.

In conclusion, polarizers play a crucial role in many areas of science and technology. Their ability to manipulate the polarization state of light makes them an essential tool in a wide range of applications, from optical instruments to telecommunications and laser systems.

## Chapter: Chapter 10: Non-uniform Plane Waves

### Introduction

In the realm of electromagnetism, the study of non-uniform plane waves is a fascinating and complex field. This chapter, Chapter 10: Non-uniform Plane Waves, delves into the intricacies of these waves, providing a comprehensive understanding of their characteristics, behavior, and applications.

Non-uniform plane waves, unlike their uniform counterparts, are waves whose properties vary across the plane of propagation. This variation can be in terms of amplitude, phase, or polarization, among other parameters. The study of these waves is crucial in many areas of science and technology, including radar systems, wireless communication, and optical devices.

In this chapter, we will explore the mathematical models that describe non-uniform plane waves. These models, often expressed in terms of vector calculus and complex numbers, provide a powerful tool for understanding and predicting the behavior of these waves. For instance, the wave equation, represented as `$\nabla^2 \mathbf{E} - \frac{1}{c^2} \frac{\partial^2 \mathbf{E}}{\partial t^2} = 0$`, where `$\mathbf{E}$` is the electric field, `$c$` is the speed of light, and `$\nabla^2$` is the Laplacian operator, is a fundamental equation in the study of non-uniform plane waves.

We will also delve into the physical interpretation of these waves. For example, the phase variation across the plane of propagation can be interpreted as a change in the path of the wave, leading to phenomena such as diffraction and interference.

By the end of this chapter, readers should have a solid understanding of non-uniform plane waves, their mathematical description, and their physical interpretation. This knowledge will serve as a foundation for further exploration into the fascinating world of electromagnetism.




#### 9.4b Polarizers

Polarizers are essential components in many optical systems, including cameras, LCD screens, and optical filters. They are used to selectively transmit light waves with a specific polarization state, and to block light waves with other polarization states. This section will delve deeper into the properties and applications of polarizers.

##### Polarizer Properties

Polarizers are typically made of birefringent materials, such as calcite or polaroid plastic. These materials have different refractive indices for light waves with different polarization states. When a light wave passes through a polarizer, it is split into two waves, each with a different polarization state. The wave with the same polarization state as the polarizer is transmitted, while the wave with the opposite polarization state is blocked.

The transmission of a polarizer can be described by a 2x2 matrix, known as the Jones matrix, which is defined as:

$$
\begin{bmatrix}
T_{xx} & T_{xy} \\
T_{yx} & T_{yy}
\end{bmatrix}
$$

where $T_{xx}$ and $T_{yy}$ are the transmission coefficients for light waves with electric field vectors in the x and y directions, respectively, and $T_{xy}$ and $T_{yx}$ are the cross-transmission coefficients.

The Jones matrix of a polarizer is typically diagonal, with $T_{xx} = T_{yy}$, and $T_{xy} = T_{yx} = 0$. This means that the polarizer transmits light waves with electric field vectors in the x and y directions with equal efficiency, and does not transmit light waves with electric field vectors in the opposite directions.

##### Polarizer Applications

Polarizers have a wide range of applications in optics and electronics. They are used in cameras to reduce glare and reflections, in LCD screens to control the polarization of light, and in optical filters to selectively transmit light waves with a specific polarization state.

In the field of electromagnetics, polarizers are used in antennas to control the polarization of electromagnetic waves. For example, a linear polarizer can be used to transmit only electromagnetic waves with a specific linear polarization state, while a circular polarizer can be used to transmit only electromagnetic waves with a specific circular polarization state.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of polarization in the context of electromagnetic waves, and how it relates to the properties and applications of polarizers.

#### 9.4c Polarizers and Polarized Light

Polarizers and polarized light are closely intertwined. As we have learned, polarizers are devices that selectively transmit light waves with a specific polarization state. When a light wave passes through a polarizer, it is split into two waves, each with a different polarization state. The wave with the same polarization state as the polarizer is transmitted, while the wave with the opposite polarization state is blocked.

Polarized light, on the other hand, is light in which the oscillations of the electric field vector are confined to a single plane. This property is a result of the light wave's interaction with a medium, such as a polarizer. The polarization state of a light wave can be represented by a Jones vector, which is a 2x1 complex vector.

The Jones vector of a light wave can be transformed by a Jones matrix, which represents the transformation of the light wave by an optical element. For a polarizer, the Jones matrix is diagonal, with the transmission coefficients for the x and y polarization states on the diagonal, and the cross-transmission coefficients off the diagonal.

The Jones matrix of a polarizer can be used to calculate the Jones vector of the transmitted light wave. This allows us to predict the behavior of a light wave when it passes through a polarizer. For example, if we know the Jones vector of a light wave, we can calculate the Jones vector of the transmitted light wave by multiplying the Jones vector by the Jones matrix of the polarizer.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of polarization in the context of electromagnetic waves. We will learn that polarization is a fundamental property of electromagnetic waves, and that it plays a crucial role in many optical and electronic systems.




#### 9.4c Polarized Light Applications

Polarized light has a wide range of applications in various fields, including optics, electronics, and telecommunications. This section will explore some of these applications in more detail.

##### Polarized Light in Optics

In optics, polarized light is used in a variety of applications, including:

- **Polarizing Filters**: Polarizing filters are used in cameras to reduce glare and reflections, and in LCD screens to control the polarization of light. They are also used in microscopes to eliminate unwanted light and improve image contrast.

- **Polarizing Microscopes**: Polarizing microscopes are used in geology, mineralogy, and materials science to study the polarization properties of different materials. This can provide valuable information about the structure and composition of these materials.

- **Polarizing Beamsplitters**: Polarizing beamsplitters are used in interferometers and other optical systems to separate light waves with different polarization states. This can be useful for studying the properties of light waves, or for creating images with different polarization states.

##### Polarized Light in Electronics

In electronics, polarized light is used in a variety of applications, including:

- **Polarized LCD Screens**: Polarized LCD screens are used in many electronic devices, including smartphones, tablets, and TVs. They use polarized light to control the polarization of the light that is displayed, allowing for high-contrast images and improved energy efficiency.

- **Polarized LEDs**: Polarized LEDs are used in applications where high-speed data transmission is required, such as in free-space optical communication systems. They can transmit data at much higher speeds than traditional LEDs, making them ideal for applications such as wireless internet access.

##### Polarized Light in Telecommunications

In telecommunications, polarized light is used in a variety of applications, including:

- **Polarized Antennas**: Polarized antennas are used in wireless communication systems to transmit and receive electromagnetic waves with a specific polarization state. This can improve the efficiency and reliability of wireless communication, especially in crowded environments where multiple signals can interfere with each other.

- **Polarized Fiber Optics**: Polarized fiber optics are used in optical communication systems to transmit large amounts of data over long distances. They can transmit data at much higher speeds than traditional copper wires, making them ideal for applications such as high-speed internet access.

In conclusion, polarized light has a wide range of applications in various fields, and understanding its properties and behavior is crucial for designing and optimizing these applications.

We have explored the fundamental principles that govern their behavior, and how these principles are applied in various practical applications. From motors to lasers, the principles of uniform electromagnetic plane waves are at the heart of many modern technologies.

We have learned that uniform electromagnetic plane waves are a type of electromagnetic wave where the electric and magnetic fields are perpendicular to each other and to the direction of propagation. This type of wave is fundamental to the operation of many devices, including motors and lasers.

We have also seen how these waves are generated and how they interact with different materials. We have learned that the interaction of these waves with materials can be used to control the behavior of these waves, and this is the basis of many practical applications.

In conclusion, the study of uniform electromagnetic plane waves is a crucial aspect of understanding the behavior of electromagnetic waves and their applications. It is a field that is constantly evolving, with new discoveries and applications being made on a regular basis.

This chapter, "Non-uniform EM Plane Waves," will introduce and explore the fascinating world of non-uniform electromagnetic plane waves.

Non-uniform electromagnetic plane waves are waves where the electric and magnetic fields are not perpendicular to each other and to the direction of propagation. These waves are characterized by their non-uniformity, which can be in the form of a varying amplitude, frequency, or phase. This non-uniformity can have significant implications on the behavior and applications of these waves.

In this chapter, we will delve into the mathematical representation of non-uniform electromagnetic plane waves.
